## Page 156

APPENDICES
154
ADAPTATION NOTES
Adapting any premise from one 
medium to another (in this case from 
novel to adventure wargame) always 
requires some careful thought about 
how to proceed�
One of the earliest decisions was to let the 
player create their own squad with their 
own personalities� If you have a favorite 
from the book, you may be surprised 
how often the dice conspire to give you 
a character that fits your favorite, but a 
big part of adventure wargaming is to 
establish a personal connection to troops 
and characters that are uniquely yours�
For a miniatures game, a challenge from 
the first novel is that the rangers spent 
a lot of time defending in place� That 
works to create very tense and exciting 
scenes in a novel, but on the tabletop, it 
would end up feeling repetitive� Instead, 
the player undertakes an array of 
missions that brings them into contact 
with the enemy� 
From these two decisions arose the idea of 
having the player’s force separated from 
the rest of the rangers and trying to fight 
their way back� This also cuts down on a 
little bit of the hardware and high-tech 
gadgetry which tends to be more fun 
in literature than in gameplay, such as 
drones and Claymore mines� 
This also explains why you may end up 
seeing completely different creatures to 
those the heroes encountered in the book� 
The novel takes place in a modest amount 
of real estate, so it would make sense that 
they only encounter certain creatures� 
When you finally meet up, your rangers 
will have quite a story to tell!
Within the realm of the Forgotten Ruin 
novels (and fantasy literature in general), 
a vast array of creatures and spells are 
possible� Since our perspective in a 
miniatures game is tactical combat, some 
cuts had to be made while other creatures 
and spells were added� 


---

## Page 157

APPENDICES
155
Similarly, the choices of creatures, 
enemies, and transformations had 
to fit the dual requirement that 
people will want to use favorite 
miniatures in their game and that 
the option should be possible to 
represent at all� The result is that I 
have used broad types that should 
be easier to make line up with a 
given figure collection�
One of the core feelings of the 
novel is the contrast between high-
powered military firepower and the 
legions of orcs and other monsters� 
Of course, we could not ask players 
to paint up hundreds of enemies for 
the game, so things needed to be scaled 
down� In turn, as your rangers do not have 
direct access to the Forge from the book, 
they are assumed to be rationing their 
ammunition and supplies, which limits 
the amount of ultra-firepower you can 
put out in a  given engagement� The M249 
machine gun is capable for a rate of fire 
exceeding 800 rounds per minute, which 
sounds great until you realize you only 
have 900 rounds left�
Similarly, while pitting armored vehicles 
against giant monsters is attractive in 
theory, such vehicles present issues of 
balance as well as tiresomely demanding 
fuel be tracked in addition to ammo� 
If you have such models to hand, you 
might opt to place the vehicle model 
on the table to serve as terrain, having 
exhausted its ammunition and fuel in an 
earlier engagement�
It was important to capture the military 
feel in different ways, however, and the 
combination of Leadership Tactics and 
heavy weapons should do so nicely� 
Being able to pull off a critical tactic 
at just the right moment is endlessly 
satisfying, particularly if you can pull off 
an unlikely win in the process�  
 


---

## Page 158

APPENDICES
156
DESIGNER NOTES
Getting a chance to write something 
you never would have thought of can be 
extremely rewarding� Forgotten Ruin is 
such a wild concept that it simultaneously 
feels both very fresh and so obvious for a 
miniatures game: High firepower versus 
the evil hordes of a fantasy world� Part 
of why it works so well for wargamers 
is the transgressive effect of combining 
miniatures that normally aren’t used 
together� Seeing a fire team of US Rangers 
face down a mob of axe wielding orcs is 
just plain fun!
Mechanically, I knew the game would use 
the core mechanics from the Five Parsecs 
/ Five Leagues games� In the end I opted 
to stick fairly close to Five Parsecs from 
Home due to the firepower-oriented game 
play� Additionally, since we were going to 
have quite a few figures on the table, I was 
cautious about how much detail would be 
too much for players to handle� 
If you are new to these mechanics, you 
should find them approachable� Pay 
particular attention to the turn sequence� 
Who moves now and who moves later 
can often have a big impact on how your 
turns play out� Experienced ‘5X’ players 
will find that this game is not completely 
symmetrical; for example, we don’t track 
Stun markers for the enemies� The Knock 
Down status is also new, allowing a bit 
more of a cinematic vibe to the game 
as combatants get thrown around or 
knocked off their feet� 
The armory is comparatively small in 
Forgotten Ruin: , but the weapons are 
generally more powerful than what 
you are used to� It is a fool's errand to 
accurately model the fire superiority of 
a modern military rifle compared to a 
bow and the game does not really attempt 
to do so� The military weapons do have 
individual niches, and successful combat 
against the hobgoblins will require 
using each weapon to its advantage� 
Concentrated firepower will win 
battles, but you must deal with two 
other concerns:
First, the enemy will not relent� The 
Aggression mechanic means that more 
reinforcements are incoming, and they 
may well be quite unpleasant� Second, 
you are here to accomplish a mission� 
Whereas in the other campaign games 
in the series you are looking at the 
longer picture, in Forgotten Ruin you 
are a soldier first and foremost� While 
the missions may involve wizards and 
dragons, they are still missions� 


---

## Page 159

APPENDICES
157
I decided to focus on the procedural 
generation for missions rather than 
creating distinct scenarios� Setting up 
a mission can be somewhat dice-heavy 
but some of the steps are optional� The 
end result should be a very wide variety 
of possible tasks and, as unexpected 
components interact with each other, 
the challenge should be fun and 
unpredictable� Of course, at some level 
any wargame mission comes down 
to “shoot the other guys”, but there is 
potentially more going on if you want to 
be successful�
The campaign follows the familiar 
structure of pre-game, battle, and 
post-game segments� The emphasis has 
shifted a little towards the battle: The 
fights are both larger (a typical encounter 
might be 9 soldiers vs 15 or so enemies) 
and there is more going on in general� A 
battle can easily escalate out of control 
when a dragon shows up� Conversely, 
the player also receives some pretty cool 
tools to play with: The combination of 
Leadership Tactics, character traits and 
knacks, transformations, spells, magic 
items, and old-fashioned firepower can 
be quite potent, and the successful player 
will combine them in interesting and 
unexpected ways�
Advancement comes in multiple 
forms� Due to the large squad size, we 
opted not to rely on experience points 
for Forgotten Ruin: The Adventure 
Wargame� This also means that the 
level of advancement will be pretty 
uniform across a campaign� In return, 
the player gets to pick who receives 
the advancement, which you can use to 
beef up a strong character or reward 
someone who had a lucky shot in the 
last fight� Transformations open up a 
whole range of opportunities for players 
to really get creative�
In this game, campaigns have a set 
duration� When you have gone through 
the last turn, you are faced with the end 
game� This feeds back into every other 
part of the game: You only have a set 
number of advances, resources, and 
transformations to obtain, and because 
your soldiers are not impervious, putting 
all your upgrades into a single soldier can 
be high-risk if they then die� Tactically 
minded players may look at the time 
frames involved and plan ahead� 
In any solo game there is a temptation to 
try to “solve” the game – any game system 
has optimal solutions and superior 
options� Some players enjoy figuring this 
out, while others prefer prioritizing the 
story and only picking the options that 
feels like they fit the best� As thousands of 
people play a game, they will inevitably 
find elements that are exploitable to 
give players an edge� While some of the 
more obvious cases can be closed off by 
the designer, if you need to game a game 
system, you don’t need to hunt down all 
of them: You are the person with a pencil 
and the character roster, so if you prefer 
to give everyone a Combat Skill of +7 that 
is all up to you! 
One important final factor that is 
perhaps easy to miss is the presence 
of character traits� This is a little more 
open-ended than people may be used 
to in a miniatures game, but is a neat 
mechanic to add flair to a tense encounter� 
As written, the mechanic comes into 
play when a 1-point difference to the roll 
would make a difference� This is a fitting 
level for something like this� 
I hope you enjoy this game and that you 
have as much fun playing it as we had 
creating it�
Peace and love�
Ivan Sørensen�

When soldiers shoot, for each Shot,

Hit Resolution
When a character takes a Hit, roll 
1D6 + the weapon’s damage rating  
+ any bonuses (e�g� Great Hit)� 
  Enemies use a +0 damage 
rating unless stated otherwise�
  Hand-to-hand combat uses 
the weapon’s damage rating� 
Soldiers without a hand-to-
hand weapon use +0 damage�
If the result is equal to or above the 
target’s Toughness, they become a 
casualty and are removed� Monsters 
lose 1 MP instead, and are removed 
from play once they lose all MPs� 
If the result is below Toughness, 
enemies and monsters are 
unaffected; soldiers receive a Stun 
marker (ignore any beyond the 3rd)�
Saving Throws
If a figure with a saving throw value 
is Hit, roll a D6:
If the roll is equal to or above the 
value, the Hit has no effect� 
If the roll is below the value, make 
the roll against Toughness during 
Hit Resolution, above� Note: soldiers 
who pass a saving throw do not 
receive a Stun marker�

MUH098V004
Printed in Lithuania
The battlefield has never looked like this!
A time of danger, a secret mission, mysterious tech, and now you and your 
platoon of rangers are in a strange world inhabited by orcs, sorcerers, and dragons!
Forgotten Ruin: The Adventure Wargame takes you to the strangest 
battlefield yet, pitting state-of-the-art firepower against fantastical monsters. 
Just hope the orc horde runs out before the ammunition does!
Based on the Forgotten Ruin book series, the rules are powered by the ‘Five X’ game system 
from Five Leagues from the Borderlands and Five Parsecs from Home, unleashing the 
same solo adventuring thrills. Command your squad while the game rules control your 
enemies as they move and fight. You can even play with a friend by splitting your squad into 
two fire teams or designating one of you to control the enemy like a Game Master.
This adventure wargame offers an exciting mix of tabletop tactics for miniature figures and 
randomly generated campaign play, providing a unique, story-rich experience.
Inside this book you will find:
  Full rules for miniatures combat on 
strange battlefields including powerful 
heavy weapons, leadership tactics to 
customise your squad, spells, and  
off-map fire support.
  Stats for an array of foes to fight:  
20 enemy forces with 6 different types 
(such as Assassins or Archers) as well 
as 20 huge monsters to challenge your 
firepower.
  Rules to turn enemy champions into 
recurring villains.
  Campaign rules, objectives, and 
rewards, including magical treasures, 
advancements, and, best of all...
  Transformations! Ever wanted to be 
a centaur with a machine gun? An elf 
sniper? Or just a squad leader with 
a spell book? Your soldiers will not 
remain the way they were and the 
changes can be quite unpredictable!
